<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">The Rolling Stones</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short plainlinks noprint" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.25wmf17/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/><img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.25wmf17/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/></div>

<p><b>The Rolling Stones</b> are an English <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a> band formed in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> in 1962. The first settled line-up consisted of <a href="/wiki/Brian_Jones" title="Brian Jones">Brian Jones</a> (guitar, harmonica), <a href="/wiki/Ian_Stewart_(musician)" title="Ian Stewart (musician)">Ian Stewart</a> (piano), <a href="/wiki/Mick_Jagger" title="Mick Jagger">Mick Jagger</a> (lead vocals, harmonica), <a href="/wiki/Keith_Richards" title="Keith Richards">Keith Richards</a> (guitar), <a href="/wiki/Bill_Wyman" title="Bill Wyman">Bill Wyman</a> (bass), and <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Watts" title="Charlie Watts">Charlie Watts</a> (drums). Stewart was removed from the official line-up in 1963 but continued as occasional pianist until his death in 1985. Jones left the band less than a month prior to his death in 1969, having already been replaced by <a href="/wiki/Mick_Taylor" title="Mick Taylor">Mick Taylor</a>, who left in 1975. Since then <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_Wood" title="Ronnie Wood">Ronnie Wood</a> has been on guitar in tandem with Richards. Following Wyman's departure in 1993, <a href="/wiki/Darryl_Jones" title="Darryl Jones">Darryl Jones</a> has been the main bassist. Other notable keyboardists for the band have included <a href="/wiki/Nicky_Hopkins" title="Nicky Hopkins">Nicky Hopkins</a>, active from 1967 to 1982; <a href="/wiki/Billy_Preston" title="Billy Preston">Billy Preston</a> through the mid 1970s (most prominent on <i><a href="/wiki/Black_and_Blue" title="Black and Blue">Black and Blue</a></i>); and <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Leavell" title="Chuck Leavell">Chuck Leavell</a>, active since 1982. The band was first led by Jones, but after teaming as the band's songwriters, Jagger and Richards assumed <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto_standard" title="De facto standard">de facto</a></i> leadership.</p>
<p>The Rolling Stones were in the vanguard of the <a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a> of bands that became popular in the US in 1964–65. At first noted for their longish hair as much as their music, the band are identified with the youthful and rebellious <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a>. Critic Sean Egan states that within a year of the release of their 1964 debut album, they "were being perceived by the youth of Britain and then the world as representatives of opposition to an old, cruel order — the antidote to a class-bound, authoritarian culture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> They were instrumental in making blues a major part of rock and roll, and of changing the international focus of blues culture to the less sophisticated blues typified by <a href="/wiki/Chess_Records" title="Chess Records">Chess Records</a> artists such as <a href="/wiki/Muddy_Waters" title="Muddy Waters">Muddy Waters</a>, writer of "<a href="/wiki/Rollin%27_Stone" title="Rollin' Stone">Rollin' Stone</a>", the song after which the band is named. After a short period of musical experimentation that culminated with the poorly received and largely <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_music" title="Psychedelic music">psychedelic</a> album <i><a href="/wiki/Their_Satanic_Majesties_Request" title="Their Satanic Majesties Request">Their Satanic Majesties Request</a></i> (1967), the group returned to its bluesy roots with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beggars%27_Banquet" title="Beggars' Banquet">Beggars' Banquet</a></i> (1968) which – along with its follow-ups, <i><a href="/wiki/Let_It_Bleed" title="Let It Bleed">Let It Bleed</a></i> (1969), <i><a href="/wiki/Sticky_Fingers" title="Sticky Fingers">Sticky Fingers</a></i> (1971) and <i><a href="/wiki/Exile_on_Main_St." title="Exile on Main St.">Exile on Main St.</a></i> (1972) – is generally considered to be the band's best work, and are considered the Rolling Stones' "Golden Age". Musicologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Palmer_(writer)" title="Robert Palmer (writer)">Robert Palmer</a> attributed the "remarkable endurance" of the Rolling Stones to being "rooted in traditional verities, in rhythm-and-blues and soul music" while "more ephemeral pop fashions have come and gone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The band continued to release commercially successful records through the 1970s and selling many albums with <i><a href="/wiki/Some_Girls" title="Some Girls">Some Girls</a></i> (1978) and <i><a href="/wiki/Tattoo_You" title="Tattoo You">Tattoo You</a></i> (1981) being their two most sold albums worldwide. In the 1980s, a feud between Jagger and Richards about the band's musical direction almost caused the band to split but they managed to patch their relationship and had a big comeback with <i><a href="/wiki/Steel_Wheels" title="Steel Wheels">Steel Wheels</a></i> (1989) which was followed by a big stadium and arena tour. Since the 1990s, new recorded material from the group has been both increasingly less well-received and less frequent. Despite this, the Stones have continued to be a huge attraction on the live circuit with big stadium tours continuing through the 1990s and 2000s. By 2007, the band had made what were then four of the top five <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_concert_tours" title="List of highest-grossing concert tours">highest-grossing concert tours</a> of all time (<a href="/wiki/Voodoo_Lounge_Tour" title="Voodoo Lounge Tour">Voodoo Lounge Tour</a> (1994–95), <a href="/wiki/Bridges_to_Babylon_Tour" title="Bridges to Babylon Tour">Bridges to Babylon Tour</a> (1997–99), <a href="/wiki/Licks_Tour" title="Licks Tour">Licks Tour</a> (2002–03) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Bigger_Bang_Tour" title="A Bigger Bang Tour">A Bigger Bang Tour</a> (2005–07)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2010141_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2010141-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Rolling Stones were inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> in 1989, and the <a href="/wiki/UK_Music_Hall_of_Fame" title="UK Music Hall of Fame">UK Music Hall of Fame</a> in 2004. <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine ranked them fourth on the "100 Greatest Artists of All Time" list, and their <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_music_artists" title="List of best-selling music artists">estimated album sales</a> are above 250 million. They have <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_discography" title="The Rolling Stones discography">released</a> twenty-nine studio albums, eighteen live albums and numerous compilations. <i><a href="/wiki/Let_It_Bleed" title="Let It Bleed">Let It Bleed</a></i> (1969) was their first of five consecutive number one studio and live albums in the UK. <i><a href="/wiki/Sticky_Fingers" title="Sticky Fingers">Sticky Fingers</a></i> (1971) was the first of eight consecutive number one studio albums in the US. In 2008 the band ranked 10th on the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100_All-Time_Top_Artists#All-Time_top_artists" title="Billboard Hot 100 All-Time Top Artists"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 All-Time Top Artists</a>" chart. In 2012, the band celebrated its 50th anniversary.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Rolling_Stones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Rolling_Stones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Keith_Richards" title="Keith Richards">Keith Richards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mick_Jagger" title="Mick Jagger">Mick Jagger</a> were childhood friends and classmates in <a href="/wiki/Dartford" title="Dartford">Dartford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, until the Jaggers moved to <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Kent" title="Wilmington, Kent">Wilmington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20108_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20108-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Jagger had formed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garage_band" title="Garage band">garage band</a> with <a href="/wiki/Dick_Taylor" title="Dick Taylor">Dick Taylor</a>, mainly playing <a href="/wiki/Muddy_Waters" title="Muddy Waters">Muddy Waters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_Richard" title="Little Richard">Little Richard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Howlin%27_Wolf" title="Howlin' Wolf">Howlin' Wolf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bo_Diddley" title="Bo Diddley">Bo Diddley</a> material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20108_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20108-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Jagger was reacquainted with Keith Richards in 1960 at <a href="/wiki/Dartford_railway_station" title="Dartford railway station">Dartford railway station</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20109_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20109-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The Chuck Berry and Muddy Waters records that Jagger carried revealed a common interest that prompted their musical partnership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20109_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20109-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenfield1981_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenfield1981-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Richards joined Jagger and Taylor at frequent meetings at Jagger's house. The meetings switched to Taylor's house in late 1961, where the three were joined by Alan Etherington and Bob Beckwith. They called themselves The Blue Boys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201010.E2.80.9311_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201010.E2.80.9311-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1962, the Blue Boys read about the <a href="/wiki/Ealing_Jazz_Club" title="Ealing Jazz Club">Ealing Jazz Club</a> in newspaper <i>Jazz News</i> and visited the place on 7 April 1962. The band members met <a href="/wiki/Brian_Jones" title="Brian Jones">Brian Jones</a> there, as he sat in playing <a href="/wiki/Slide_guitar" title="Slide guitar">slide guitar</a> with <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Korner" title="Alexis Korner">Alexis Korner</a>'s seminal London <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">rhythm and blues</a> band, <a href="/wiki/Blues_Incorporated" title="Blues Incorporated">Blues Incorporated</a>, the band that also had future Rolling Stones members <a href="/wiki/Ian_Stewart_(musician)" title="Ian Stewart (musician)">Ian Stewart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Watts" title="Charlie Watts">Charlie Watts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201011_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201011-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Before visiting the Ealing Jazz Club, the Blue Boys had sent a tape of their best recordings to Alexis Korner, who was impressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201011_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201011-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> After a meeting with Korner, Jagger and Richards started jamming with Blues Incorporated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201011_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201011-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Brian Jones advertised for band mates in the <i>Jazz News</i> and Ian Stewart found a practice space and joined with Jones to start a rhythm and blues band playing <a href="/wiki/Chicago_blues" title="Chicago blues">Chicago blues</a>. Shortly thereafter, Jagger, Taylor and Richards left Blues Incorporated to join Jones and Stewart in their effort. Also at the first rehearsal were guitarist <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Bradford_(musician)" title="Geoff Bradford (musician)">Geoff Bradford</a> and vocalist Brian Knight, both of whom declined to join the band citing objections to playing the Chuck Berry and Bo Diddley songs preferred by Jagger and Richards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200340_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200340-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> In June 1962 the line-up was: Jagger, Jones, Richards, Stewart, Taylor, and drummer <a href="/wiki/Tony_Chapman" title="Tony Chapman">Tony Chapman</a>. According to Richards, Jones christened the band during a phone call to <i>Jazz News</i>. When asked for a band name Jones saw a Muddy Waters LP lying on the floor of which one of the tracks was "<a href="/wiki/Rollin%27_Stone" title="Rollin' Stone">Rollin' Stone</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200342_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200342-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201013_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201013-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1962.E2.80.9364:_Building_a_following">1962–64: Building a following</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Rolling_Stones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1962–64: Building a following">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6016" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg/220px-Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg/330px-Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg/440px-Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crawdaddy_club_richmond_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The back room of what was the <a href="/wiki/Crawdaddy_Club" title="Crawdaddy Club">Crawdaddy Club</a> in Richmond, London where the Rolling Stones had their first residency in 1963</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jagger, Richards and Jones with Stewart and <a href="/wiki/Dick_Taylor" title="Dick Taylor">Dick Taylor</a> on bass billed as "The Rollin' Stones" played their first gig on 12 July 1962, at the <a href="/wiki/Marquee_Club" title="Marquee Club">Marquee Club</a>, 165 Oxford Street, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman200236.E2.80.9337_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman200236.E2.80.9337-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Their material included the Chicago blues as well as Chuck Berry and Bo Diddley songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBockris199242.E2.80.9343_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBockris199242.E2.80.9343-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Bassist <a href="/wiki/Bill_Wyman" title="Bill Wyman">Bill Wyman</a> joined in December 1962 and drummer Charlie Watts the following January 1963 to form the band's long-standing <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_section" title="Rhythm section">rhythm section</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201014.E2.80.9315_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201014.E2.80.9315-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman200240.E2.80.9341.3B_44.E2.80.9345_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman200240.E2.80.9341.3B_44.E2.80.9345-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The Rolling Stones' then acting manager <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Gomelsky" title="Giorgio Gomelsky">Giorgio Gomelsky</a> secured a Sunday afternoon residency at the <a href="/wiki/Crawdaddy_Club" title="Crawdaddy Club">Crawdaddy Club</a> in Richmond, which, Gomelsky said, triggered an "international renaissance for the blues" and was a seminal facet of <a href="/wiki/Swinging_London" title="Swinging London">Swinging London's</a> advent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200350.E2.80.9351_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200350.E2.80.9351-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Rolling Stones signed manager <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Loog_Oldham" title="Andrew Loog Oldham">Andrew Loog Oldham</a>, a publicist who was directed to the band by previous clients, <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201020_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201020-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Because Oldham had not reached majority – he was nineteen and younger than any member of the band – he could not get an agent's licence nor sign any contracts without his mother also signing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201020_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201020-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> By necessity he joined with booking agent Eric Easton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman1990123_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman1990123-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000223_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000223-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman200256.E2.80.9357_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman200256.E2.80.9357-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Gomelsky had no written agreement with the band and was not consulted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman1990135.E2.80.93136_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman1990135.E2.80.93136-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Oldham made several changes to the band. He changed the spelling of the band name from "the Rollin' Stones" to "the Rolling Stones". He removed the s from Richards' last name saying it "looked more pop".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBockris199263_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBockris199263-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000222_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000222-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Oldham also removed Stewart from the line-up. Wyman said Stewart did not fit Oldham's mould of "pretty, thin, long-haired boys". Stewart stayed on as road manager, playing piano on many studio tracks and on stage until his death in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000222.E2.80.93225_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000222.E2.80.93225-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200362_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200362-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca Records</a>, which had passed on signing the Beatles, gave the Rolling Stones a recording contract with very favourable terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201022_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201022-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> They got three times a new act's typical royalty rate, full artistic control of recordings, and ownership of the recording masters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000205.2C_212_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000205.2C_212-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200368_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200368-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> The deal also let the band use non-Decca recording studios. Regent Sound Studios, a mono facility equipped with egg boxes on the ceiling for sound treatment, became the preferred facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000209.E2.80.93210.2C_212_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000209.E2.80.93210.2C_212-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoralHinckleyRodman1995_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoralHinckleyRodman1995-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Oldham, who had no recording experience but made himself the band's producer, said Regent had a sound that "leaked, instrument-to-instrument, the right way" creating a "wall of noise" that worked well for the band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200368_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200368-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000252.E2.80.93253_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000252.E2.80.93253-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Due to Regent's low rates, the band could record for extended periods rather than the usual three-hour blocks then prevalent at other studios. All tracks on the first Rolling Stones UK album were recorded at Regent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201026_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201026-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000213_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000213-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Oldham contrasted the Rolling Stones' independence with the Beatles' obligation to record in <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a>'s studios, saying it made them appear as "mere mortals ... sweating in the studio for the man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000205_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000205-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Oldham promoted the Rolling Stones as the nasty opposites of the Beatles by having the band pose unsmiling on the cover of the first UK album. He also encouraged the press to use provocative headlines such as "Would you let your daughter marry a Rolling Stone?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall201222_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall201222-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Though Oldham initially had dressed the band in uniform suits, the band drifted back to wearing everyday clothes for public appearances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman1990136_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman1990136-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Wyman: "Our reputation and image as the Bad Boys came later, completely there, accidentally. Andrew never did engineer it. He simply exploited it exhaustively".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman1990133_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman1990133-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>"we were the first pop group to break away from the whole <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Richard" title="Cliff Richard">Cliff Richard</a> thing where the bands did little dance steps, wore identical uniforms and had snappy patter". – Bill Wyman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A cover of Chuck Berry's "<a href="/wiki/Come_On_(Chuck_Berry_song)" title="Come On (Chuck Berry song)">Come On</a>" was the Rolling Stones' first single, released on 7 June 1963. The Rolling Stones refused to play it at live gigs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> and Decca bought only one ad to promote the single. With Oldham's direction fan-club members bought copies at record shops polled by the charts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000221_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000221-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> helping "Come On" rise to No.21 on the UK singles charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Having a charting single gave the band entree to play outside London, starting with a booking at the Outlook Club in Middlesbrough on 13 July, sharing the billing with <a href="/wiki/The_Hollies" title="The Hollies">the Hollies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> In Bill Wyman's book, "Rolling With The Stones" he incorrectly says the band played the Alcove club that night. Later in the year Oldham and Easton arranged the band's first big UK concert tour as a supporting act for American stars including Bo Diddley, <a href="/wiki/Little_Richard" title="Little Richard">Little Richard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Everly_Brothers" title="The Everly Brothers">The Everly Brothers</a>. This Autumn 1963 tour became a "training ground" for the young band's stagecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200368_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200368-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman200280.E2.80.9383_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman200280.E2.80.9383-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bluesbrothers_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-bluesbrothers-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During this tour the Rolling Stones recorded their second single, a <a href="/wiki/Lennon%E2%80%93McCartney" title="Lennon–McCartney">Lennon–McCartney</a>-penned number entitled "<a href="/wiki/I_Wanna_Be_Your_Man" title="I Wanna Be Your Man">I Wanna Be Your Man</a>";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_30.2C_track_2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_30.2C_track_2-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> it reached No.12 in the UK charts. Their third single, <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Holly" title="Buddy Holly">Buddy Holly</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Not_Fade_Away_(song)" title="Not Fade Away (song)">Not Fade Away</a>", itself based on Bo Diddley's style, was released in February 1964 and reached No. 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_Charts_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-UK_Charts-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Oldham saw little future for an act that lost significant songwriting <a href="/wiki/Royalties" title="Royalties">royalties</a> by playing songs of "middle-aged blacks," limiting the appeal to teenage audiences. At Oldham's urging, Jagger and Richards co-wrote songs, the first batch of which he described as "soppy and imitative."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOldham2000256.E2.80.93257_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOldham2000256.E2.80.93257-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Because songwriting developed slowly, songs on the band's first album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_(album)" title="The Rolling Stones (album)">The Rolling Stones</a></i>, (issued in the US as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/England%27s_Newest_Hit_Makers" title="England's Newest Hit Makers">England's Newest Hit Makers</a></i>) were primarily covers, with only one Jagger/Richards original – "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tell_Me_(You%27re_Coming_Back)" title="Tell Me (You're Coming Back)">Tell Me (You're Coming Back)</a>" – and two numbers credited to <a href="/wiki/Nanker_Phelge" title="Nanker Phelge">Nanker Phelge</a>, the pen name for songs written by the entire group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200384_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200384-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Rolling Stones' first US tour, in June 1964, was, in Bill Wyman's words, "a disaster." "When we arrived, we didn't have a hit record [there] or anything going for us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002126_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002126-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> When the band appeared on the variety show <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hollywood_Palace" title="The Hollywood Palace">The Hollywood Palace</a></i>, that week's guest host <a href="/wiki/Dean_Martin" title="Dean Martin">Dean Martin</a> mocked both their hair and their performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> During the tour they recorded for two days at Chess Studios in Chicago, meeting many of their most important influences, including Muddy Waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002128.E2.80.93129_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002128.E2.80.93129-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002158_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002158-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> These sessions included what would become the Rolling Stones' first number 1 hit in the UK: their cover of <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Womack" title="Bobby Womack">Bobby and Shirley Womack</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/It%27s_All_Over_Now" title="It's All Over Now">It's All Over Now</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002137_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002137-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>"The Stones" followed <a href="/wiki/James_Brown" title="James Brown">James Brown</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/The_Famous_Flames" title="The Famous Flames">The Famous Flames</a> in the filmed theatrical release of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_TAMI_Show" title="The TAMI Show">The TAMI Show</a></i>, which showcased American acts with British Invasion artists. According to Jagger in 2003, "We weren't actually following James Brown because there was considerable time between the filming of each section. Nevertheless, he was still very annoyed about it ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200388_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200388-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> On 25 October, the band also appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ed_Sullivan_Show" title="The Ed Sullivan Show">The Ed Sullivan Show</a></i>. Because of the initial pandemonium the Rolling Stones caused, Sullivan banned the band from his show,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002154_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002154-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> though they were booked for subsequent appearances in the years following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rockhall_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-rockhall-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Their second LP – the US-only <i><a href="/wiki/12_X_5" title="12 X 5">12 X 5</a></i> – was released during this tour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tioos-discography_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-tioos-discography-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Rolling Stones' fifth UK single – a cover of <a href="/wiki/Willie_Dixon" title="Willie Dixon">Willie Dixon's</a> "<a href="/wiki/Little_Red_Rooster" title="Little Red Rooster">Little Red Rooster</a>" backed by "Off the Hook" credited to Nanker Phelge – was released in November 1964 and became their second No.1 hit in the UK – an unprecedented achievement for a blues number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_Charts_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-UK_Charts-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> The band's US distributors (<a href="/wiki/London_Records" title="London Records">London Records</a>) declined to release "Little Red Rooster" as a single there. In December 1964 London Records released the band's first single with Jagger/Richards originals on both sides: "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_of_Stone_(Rolling_Stones_song)" title="Heart of Stone (Rolling Stones song)">Heart of Stone</a>" backed with "What a Shame"; "Heart of Stone" went to number 19 in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002159_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002159-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1965.E2.80.9367:_Height_of_fame">1965–67: Height of fame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Rolling_Stones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1965–67: Height of fame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="800" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg/220px-Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg/330px-Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg/440px-Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rolling_Stones_band_1965.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Rolling Stones performing at Georgia Southern College in Statesboro, Georgia, May 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The band's second UK LP – <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_No._2" title="The Rolling Stones No. 2">The Rolling Stones No. 2</a></i>, released in January 1965, charted at number 1 as an album, and the US version, released in February as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones,_Now!" title="The Rolling Stones, Now!">The Rolling Stones, Now!</a></i>, reached number 5. The album was recorded at Chess Studios in Chicago and <a href="/wiki/RCA_Records" title="RCA Records">RCA Studios</a> in Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002164.E2.80.93165.3B_171_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002164.E2.80.93165.3B_171-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> In January/February 1965 the band played 34 shows for about 100,000 people in Australia and New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002166_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002166-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first Jagger/Richards composition to reach number 1 on the UK singles charts was "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Last_Time_(song)" title="The Last Time (song)">The Last Time</a>" (released in February 1965).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_Charts_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-UK_Charts-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> it went to number 9 in the US. It was also later identified by Richards as "the bridge into thinking about writing for the Stones. It gave us a level of confidence; a pathway of how to do it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200395_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200395-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Their first international number 1 hit was "<a href="/wiki/(I_Can%27t_Get_No)_Satisfaction" title="(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction">(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction</a>", recorded in May 1965 during the band's third North American tour. In recording the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riff" title="Riff">guitar riff</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">fuzzbox</a> that drives the song, Richards had envisioned it as a <a href="/wiki/Studio_recording#The_process" title="Studio recording">scratch track</a> to guide a horn section. Disagreeing, Oldham released "Satisfaction" without the planned horn overdubs. Issued in the summer of 1965, it was their fourth UK No. 1 and first US No. 1 where it spent four weeks at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>, establishing the Rolling Stones as a worldwide premier act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200395_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood200395-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002187_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002187-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stones_ad_1965-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="287" data-file-width="245" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stones_ad_1965-2.jpg/170px-Stones_ad_1965-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Stones_ad_1965-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Stones_ad_1965-2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stones_ad_1965-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A trade ad for the 1965 Rolling Stones' North American tour.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The US version of the LP <i><a href="/wiki/Out_of_Our_Heads" title="Out of Our Heads">Out of Our Heads</a></i> (released in July 1965) also went to number 1; it included seven original songs (three Jagger/Richards numbers and four credited to Nanker Phelge).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002195_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002195-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Their second international number-1 single, "<a href="/wiki/Get_Off_of_My_Cloud" title="Get Off of My Cloud">Get Off of My Cloud</a>" was released in the autumn of 1965,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rockhall_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-rockhall-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> followed by another US-only LP: <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/December%27s_Children" title="December's Children">December's Children</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tioos-discography_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-tioos-discography-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aftermath_(Rolling_Stones_album)" title="Aftermath (Rolling Stones album)">Aftermath</a></i> (UK number 1; US 2), released in the late spring of 1966, was the first Rolling Stones album to be composed entirely of Jagger/Richards songs. On this album Jones' contributions expanded beyond guitar and harmonica. To the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_music" title="Middle Eastern music">Middle Eastern</a>-influenced "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paint_It,_Black" title="Paint It, Black">Paint It, Black</a>" he added sitar, to the ballad "<a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_(song)" title="Lady Jane (song)">Lady Jane</a>" he added <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_dulcimer" title="Appalachian dulcimer">dulcimer</a>, and to "<a href="/wiki/Under_My_Thumb" title="Under My Thumb">Under My Thumb</a>" he added <a href="/wiki/Marimba" title="Marimba">marimbas</a>. <i>Aftermath</i> was also notable for the almost 12-minute long "<a href="/wiki/Goin%27_Home_(The_Rolling_Stones_song)" title="Goin' Home (The Rolling Stones song)">Goin' Home</a>".</p>
<p>The Stones' success on the British and American singles charts peaked during 1966. "<a href="/wiki/19th_Nervous_Breakdown" title="19th Nervous Breakdown">19th Nervous Breakdown</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_38.2C_track_3_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_38.2C_track_3-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> (Feb. 1966, UK number 2, US number 2) was followed by their trans-Atlantic number-1 hit "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paint_It,_Black" title="Paint It, Black">Paint It, Black</a>" (May 1966).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_Charts_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-UK_Charts-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Mother%27s_Little_Helper" title="Mother's Little Helper">Mother's Little Helper</a>" (June 1966) reached number 8 in the US; it was one of the first pop songs to address the issue of prescription drug abuse.</p>
<p>The September 1966 single "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Have_You_Seen_Your_Mother,_Baby,_Standing_In_The_Shadow%3F" title="Have You Seen Your Mother, Baby, Standing In The Shadow?">Have You Seen Your Mother, Baby, Standing In The Shadow?</a>" (UK number 5, US number 9) was notable in several respects: It was the first Stones recording to feature brass horns, the (now-famous) back-cover photo on the original US picture sleeve depicted the group satirically dressed in <a href="/wiki/Drag_(clothing)" title="Drag (clothing)">drag</a>, and the song was accompanied by one of the first purpose-made promotional film clips (music videos), directed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Whitehead_(filmmaker)" title="Peter Whitehead (filmmaker)">Peter Whitehead</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small-left" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;float:left">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>

</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing these files? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>January 1967 saw the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Between_the_Buttons" title="Between the Buttons">Between the Buttons</a></i> (UK number 3; US 2); the album was Andrew Oldham's last venture as the Rolling Stones' producer (his role as the band's manager had been taken over by <a href="/wiki/Allen_Klein" title="Allen Klein">Allen Klein</a> in 1965). The US version included the double A-side single "<a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Spend_the_Night_Together" title="Let's Spend the Night Together">Let's Spend the Night Together</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Ruby_Tuesday_(song)" title="Ruby Tuesday (song)">Ruby Tuesday</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_46_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_46-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> which went to number 1 in the US and number 3 in the UK. When the band went to New York to perform the numbers on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ed_Sullivan_Show" title="The Ed Sullivan Show">The Ed Sullivan Show</a></i>, they were ordered to change the lyrics of the refrain to "let's spend some time together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stonemag_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-stonemag-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002256_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002256-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1967, Jagger, Richards and Jones began to be hounded by authorities over their <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">recreational drug use</a>, after <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i> ran a three-part feature entitled "Pop Stars and Drugs: Facts That Will Shock You". The series described alleged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD">LSD</a> parties hosted by <a href="/wiki/The_Moody_Blues" title="The Moody Blues">The Moody Blues</a> and attended by top stars including <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">Pete Townshend</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ginger_Baker" title="Ginger Baker">Ginger Baker</a>, and alleged admissions of drug use by leading pop musicians. The first article targeted <a href="/wiki/Donovan" title="Donovan">Donovan</a> (who was raided and charged soon after); the second instalment (published on 5 February) targeted the Rolling Stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaytress2003116_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaytress2003116-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A reporter who contributed to the story spent an evening at the exclusive London club Blaise's, where a member of the Rolling Stones allegedly took several <a href="/wiki/Benzedrine" title="Benzedrine">Benzedrine</a> tablets, displayed a piece of <a href="/wiki/Hashish" title="Hashish">hashish</a> and invited his companions back to his flat for a "smoke". The article claimed that this was Mick Jagger, but it turned out to be a case of mistaken identity—the reporter had in fact been eavesdropping on Brian Jones. On the night the article was published Jagger appeared on the <a href="/wiki/Eamonn_Andrews" title="Eamonn Andrews">Eamonn Andrews</a> chat show and announced that he was filing a writ for libel against the paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaytress2003116_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaytress2003116-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brian_Jones_1965.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="796" data-file-width="554" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Brian_Jones_1965.jpg/170px-Brian_Jones_1965.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Brian_Jones_1965.jpg/255px-Brian_Jones_1965.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Brian_Jones_1965.jpg/340px-Brian_Jones_1965.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brian_Jones_1965.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brian Jones, 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A week later on Sunday 12 February, Sussex police, tipped off by the <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i>, who in turn were tipped off by Richards' chauffeur,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guard_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-guard-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> raided a party at Keith Richards' home, Redlands. No arrests were made at the time but Jagger, Richards and their friend <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fraser_(art_dealer)" title="Robert Fraser (art dealer)">Robert Fraser</a> (an art dealer) were subsequently charged with drugs offences. Richards said in 2003, "When we got busted at Redlands, it suddenly made us realise that this was a whole different ball game and that was when the fun stopped. Up until then it had been as though London existed in a beautiful space where you could do anything you wanted."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003112_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003112-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> On the treatment of the man responsible for the raid he later added: "As I heard it, he never walked the same again."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guard_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-guard-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In March, while awaiting the consequences of the police raid, Jagger, Richards and Jones took a short trip to <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Faithfull" title="Marianne Faithfull">Marianne Faithfull</a>, Jones' girlfriend <a href="/wiki/Anita_Pallenberg" title="Anita Pallenberg">Anita Pallenberg</a> and other friends. During this trip the stormy relations between Jones and Pallenberg deteriorated to the point that Pallenberg left Morocco with Richards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002264.E2.80.93265_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002264.E2.80.93265-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Richards said later: "That was the final nail in the coffin with me and Brian. He'd never forgive me for that and I don't blame him, but hell, shit happens."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003113_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003113-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Richards and Pallenberg would remain a couple for twelve years. Despite these complications, the Rolling Stones toured Europe in March and April 1967. The tour included the band's first performances in Poland, Greece and Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002268_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002268-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 10 May 1967, the same day Jagger, Richards and Fraser were arraigned in connection with the Redlands charges—Brian Jones' house was raided by police and he was arrested and charged with possession of <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stonemag_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-stonemag-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Three out of five Rolling Stones now faced drug charges. Jagger and Richards were tried at the end of June. On 31 July Jagger was sentenced to three months' imprisonment for possession of four <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a> tablets; Richards was found guilty of allowing cannabis to be smoked on his property and sentenced to one year in prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooth2000276_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooth2000276-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Both Jagger and Richards were imprisoned at that point, but were released on bail the next day pending appeal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002278.E2.80.93282_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002278.E2.80.93282-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> ran the famous editorial entitled "<a href="/wiki/Who_breaks_a_butterfly_upon_a_wheel%3F" title="Who breaks a butterfly upon a wheel?">Who breaks a butterfly on a wheel?</a>" in which conservative editor <a href="/wiki/William_Rees-Mogg" title="William Rees-Mogg">William Rees-Mogg</a> surprised his readers by his unusually critical discourse on the sentencing, pointing out that Jagger had been treated far more harshly for a minor first offence than "any purely anonymous young man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While awaiting the appeal hearings, the band recorded a new single, "<a href="/wiki/We_Love_You" title="We Love You">We Love You</a>", as a thank-you for the loyalty shown by their fans. It began with the sound of prison doors closing, and the accompanying music video included allusions to the trial of <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> On 31 July, the appeals court overturned Richards' conviction, and Jagger's sentence was reduced to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conditional_discharge" title="Conditional discharge">conditional discharge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002286_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002286-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Brian Jones' trial took place in November 1967; in December, after appealing the original prison sentence, Jones was fined £1000, put on three years' probation and ordered to seek professional help.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002292.E2.80.93293.3B_299_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002292.E2.80.93293.3B_299-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>December 1967 also saw the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Their_Satanic_Majesties_Request" title="Their Satanic Majesties Request">Their Satanic Majesties Request</a></i> (UK number 3; US 2), released shortly after The Beatles' <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band">Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stonemag_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-stonemag-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_46_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_46-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Satanic Majesties</i> had been recorded in difficult circumstances while Jagger, Richards and Jones were dealing with their court cases. The band parted ways with producer Andrew Oldham during the sessions. The split was amicable, at least publicly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002290_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002290-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> but in 2003 Jagger said: "The reason Andrew left was because he thought that we weren't concentrating and that we were being childish. It was not a great moment really – and I would have thought it wasn't a great moment for Andrew either. There were a lot of distractions and you always need someone to focus you at that point, that was Andrew's job."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stonemag_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-stonemag-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Satanic Majesties</i> thus became the first album the Rolling Stones produced on their own. Its <a href="/wiki/Psychedelia" title="Psychedelia">psychedelic</a> sound was complemented by the cover art, which featured a 3D photo by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Cooper_(photographer)" title="Michael Cooper (photographer)">Michael Cooper</a>, who had also photographed the cover of <i>Sgt. Pepper</i>. Bill Wyman wrote and sang a track on the album: "<a href="/wiki/In_Another_Land" title="In Another Land">In Another Land</a>", which was also released as a single, the first on which Jagger did not sing lead vocal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002296.E2.80.93298_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002296.E2.80.93298-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1968.E2.80.9372:_.22Golden_Age.22">1968–72: "Golden Age"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Rolling_Stones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title='Edit section: 1968–72: "Golden Age"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Keith-Richards_and_guitar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="362" data-file-width="316" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Keith-Richards_and_guitar.jpg/170px-Keith-Richards_and_guitar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Keith-Richards_and_guitar.jpg/255px-Keith-Richards_and_guitar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/Keith-Richards_and_guitar.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Keith-Richards_and_guitar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Keith Richards, 1972.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The band spent the first few months of 1968 working on material for their next album. Those sessions resulted in the song "<a href="/wiki/Jumpin%27_Jack_Flash" title="Jumpin' Jack Flash">Jumpin' Jack Flash</a>", released as a single in May. The song and the subsequent album, <i><a href="/wiki/Beggars_Banquet" title="Beggars Banquet">Beggars Banquet</a></i> (UK number 3; US 5), an eclectic mix of country and blues-inspired tunes, marked the band's return to their roots, and the beginning of their collaboration with producer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jimmy_Miller_(producer)" title="Jimmy Miller (producer)">Jimmy Miller</a>. It featured the lead single "<a href="/wiki/Street_Fighting_Man" title="Street Fighting Man">Street Fighting Man</a>" (which addressed the political upheavals of May 1968) and "<a href="/wiki/Sympathy_for_the_Devil" title="Sympathy for the Devil">Sympathy for the Devil</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i>Beggars Banquet</i> was well received at the time of release. Richards said, "There is a change between material on <i>Satanic Majesties</i> and <i>Beggars Banquet</i>. I'd grown sick to death of the whole Maharishi guru shit and the beads and bells. Who knows where these things come from, but I guess [the music] was a reaction to what we'd done in our time off and also that severe dose of reality. A spell in prison ... will certainly give you room for thought ... I was fucking pissed with being busted. So it was, 'Right we'll go and strip this thing down.' There's a lot of anger in the music from that period."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003114_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003114-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Richards started using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_tuning" title="Open tuning">open tunings</a> for rhythm parts (often in conjunction with a <a href="/wiki/Capo" title="Capo">capo</a>), most prominently an open-E or open-D tuning in 1968. Beginning in 1969, he often used 5-string open-G tuning (with the lower 6th string removed), as heard on the 1969 single "<a href="/wiki/Honky_Tonk_Women" title="Honky Tonk Women">Honky Tonk Women</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_Sugar_(song)" title="Brown Sugar (song)">Brown Sugar</a>" (<i><a href="/wiki/Sticky_Fingers" title="Sticky Fingers">Sticky Fingers</a></i>, 1971), "<a href="/wiki/Tumbling_Dice" title="Tumbling Dice">Tumbling Dice</a>" (capo IV), "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Happy_(Rolling_Stones_song)" title="Happy (Rolling Stones song)">Happy</a>" (capo IV) (<i><a href="/wiki/Exile_on_Main_St." title="Exile on Main St.">Exile on Main St.</a></i>, 1972), and "<a href="/wiki/Start_Me_Up" title="Start Me Up">Start Me Up</a>" (<i><a href="/wiki/Tattoo_You" title="Tattoo You">Tattoo You</a></i>, 1981).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The end of 1968 saw the filming of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_Rock_and_Roll_Circus" title="The Rolling Stones Rock and Roll Circus">The Rolling Stones Rock and Roll Circus</a></i>. It featured <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono" title="Yoko Ono">Yoko Ono</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Dirty_Mac" title="The Dirty Mac">The Dirty Mac</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jethro_Tull_(band)" title="Jethro Tull (band)">Jethro Tull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Faithfull" title="Marianne Faithfull">Marianne Faithfull</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal_(musician)" title="Taj Mahal (musician)">Taj Mahal</a>. The footage was shelved for twenty-eight years but was finally released officially in 1996,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> with a <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVD</a> version released in October 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the release of <i>Beggars Banquet</i>, Brian Jones was increasingly troubled and was only sporadically contributing to the band. Jagger said that Jones was "not psychologically suited to this way of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003128_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaggerRichardsWattsWood2003128-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> His drug use had become a hindrance, and he was unable to obtain a US <a href="/wiki/Visa_(document)" title="Visa (document)">visa</a>. Richards reported that, in a June meeting with Jagger, Richards, and Watts at Jones' house, Jones admitted that he was unable to "go on the road again". Richards said all agreed to let Jones "... say 'I've left, and if I want to I can come back'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenfield1981_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenfield1981-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> On 3 July 1969, less than a month later, Jones drowned in the swimming pool under mysterious circumstances at his home, Cotchford Farm, in <a href="/wiki/Hartfield" title="Hartfield">Hartfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Sussex" title="East Sussex">East Sussex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mick_Taylor2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="573" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mick_Taylor2.jpg/220px-Mick_Taylor2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mick_Taylor2.jpg/330px-Mick_Taylor2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mick_Taylor2.jpg/440px-Mick_Taylor2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mick_Taylor2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mick Taylor, 1972.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Rolling Stones were scheduled to play at a free concert for <a href="/wiki/Blackhill_Enterprises" title="Blackhill Enterprises">Blackhill Enterprises</a> in London's <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park</a>, two days after Brian Jones' death; they decided to proceed with the show as a tribute to Jones. The concert, their first with <a href="/wiki/Mick_Taylor" title="Mick Taylor">Mick Taylor</a>, was performed in front of an estimated 250,000 fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stonemag_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-stonemag-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The performance was filmed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granada_Television" title="Granada Television">Granada Television</a> production team, and was shown on British television as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stones_in_the_Park" title="The Stones in the Park">The Stones in the Park</a></i>. Jagger read an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Shelley</a>'s poem <i><a href="/wiki/Adona%C3%AFs" title="Adonaïs">Adonaïs</a></i>, an elegy written on the death of his friend <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">John Keats</a>, and they released thousands of butterflies in memory of Jones<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stonemag_68-5"><a href="#cite_note-stonemag-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> before opening their set with "I'm Yours and I'm Hers", a <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Winter" title="Johnny Winter">Johnny Winter</a> number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StonesinthePark_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-StonesinthePark-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Also performed, but previously unheard by the audience, were "<a href="/wiki/Midnight_Rambler" title="Midnight Rambler">Midnight Rambler</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Love_In_Vain" title="Love In Vain">Love In Vain</a>" from their forthcoming album <i><a href="/wiki/Let_It_Bleed" title="Let It Bleed">Let It Bleed</a></i> (released December 1969) and "<a href="/wiki/Loving_Cup_(song)" title="Loving Cup (song)">Give Me A Drink</a>" which eventually appeared on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exile_On_Main_St." title="Exile On Main St.">Exile On Main Street</a></i> (released May 1972). The show also included the concert debut of "<a href="/wiki/Honky_Tonk_Women" title="Honky Tonk Women">Honky Tonk Women</a>", which the band had just released the previous day. The Blackhill Enterprises stage manager <a href="/wiki/Sam_Cutler" title="Sam Cutler">Sam Cutler</a> introduced them as "the greatest rock &amp; roll band in the world",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StonesinthePark_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-StonesinthePark-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> a description he repeated throughout their <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_American_Tour_1969" title="The Rolling Stones American Tour 1969">1969 US tour</a>, and which has stuck to this day (Cutler left Blackhill Enterprises to become the Stones' road manager following the Hyde Park concert).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>

<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing these files? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The release of <i><a href="/wiki/Let_It_Bleed" title="Let It Bleed">Let It Bleed</a></i> (UK number 1; US 3) came in December. Their last album of the sixties, <i>Let It Bleed</i> featured "<a href="/wiki/Gimme_Shelter" title="Gimme Shelter">Gimme Shelter</a>". The lead female vocalist – and famed solo – on "Gimme Shelter" is performed by singer <a href="/wiki/Merry_Clayton" title="Merry Clayton">Merry Clayton</a>, (sister of <a href="/wiki/Sam_Clayton" title="Sam Clayton">Sam Clayton</a>, of the American rock band, <a href="/wiki/Little_Feat" title="Little Feat">Little Feat</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Other tracks include "<a href="/wiki/You_Can%27t_Always_Get_What_You_Want" title="You Can't Always Get What You Want">You Can't Always Get What You Want</a>" (with accompaniment by the London Bach Choir, who initially asked for their name to be removed from the album's credits after being apparently 'horrified' by the content of some of its other material, but later withdrew this request), "<a href="/wiki/Midnight_Rambler" title="Midnight Rambler">Midnight Rambler</a>" as well as a cover of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Johnson_(musician)" title="Robert Johnson (musician)">Robert Johnson</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Love_in_Vain" title="Love in Vain">Love in Vain</a>". Jones and Taylor are featured on two tracks each.</p>
<p>Just after the tour the band performed at the <a href="/wiki/Altamont_Free_Concert" title="Altamont Free Concert">Altamont Free Concert</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altamont_Speedway" title="Altamont Speedway">Altamont Speedway</a>, about 50 miles east of San Francisco. The biker gang <a href="/wiki/Hells_Angels" title="Hells Angels">Hells Angels</a> provided security, and a fan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meredith_Hunter" title="Meredith Hunter">Meredith Hunter</a>, was stabbed and beaten to death by the Angels after they realised that he was armed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Part of the tour and the Altamont concert were documented in <a href="/wiki/Albert_and_David_Maysles" title="Albert and David Maysles">Albert and David Maysles</a>' film <i><a href="/wiki/Gimme_Shelter_(1970_film)" title="Gimme Shelter (1970 film)">Gimme Shelter</a></i>. As a response to the growing popularity of <a href="/wiki/Bootleg_recording" title="Bootleg recording">bootleg recordings</a> (in particular the still sought-after <i><a href="/wiki/Live%27r_Than_You%27ll_Ever_Be" title="Live'r Than You'll Ever Be">Live'r Than You'll Ever Be</a></i>), the album <i><a href="/wiki/Get_Yer_Ya-Ya%27s_Out!_The_Rolling_Stones_in_Concert" title="Get Yer Ya-Ya's Out! The Rolling Stones in Concert">Get Yer Ya-Yas Out!</a></i> (UK 1; US 6) was released in 1970; it was declared by critic <a href="/wiki/Lester_Bangs" title="Lester Bangs">Lester Bangs</a> to be the best live album ever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the turn of the decade the band appeared on the BBC's highly rated review of the sixties music scene <i><a href="/wiki/Pop_Go_The_Sixties" title="Pop Go The Sixties">Pop Go The Sixties</a></i>, performing "Gimme Shelter" on the show, which was broadcast live on 31 December 1969. In 1970 the band's contracts with both <a href="/wiki/Allen_Klein" title="Allen Klein">Allen Klein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca Records</a> ended (cf. "<a href="/wiki/Schoolboy_Blues" title="Schoolboy Blues">Schoolboy Blues</a>"), and amid contractual disputes with Klein, they formed their own record company, <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stones_Records" title="Rolling Stones Records">Rolling Stones Records</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Sticky_Fingers" title="Sticky Fingers">Sticky Fingers</a></i> (UK number 1; US 1), released in March 1971, the band's first album on their own label, featured an elaborate cover design by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> The Stones' Decca catalogue is currently owned by Klein's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABKCO" title="ABKCO">ABKCO</a> label.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:113px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Rolling_Stones_Tongue_Logo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="127" data-file-width="111" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/02/The_Rolling_Stones_Tongue_Logo.png" width="111"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Rolling_Stones_Tongue_Logo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Rolling Stones' logo, designed by <a href="/wiki/John_Pasche" title="John Pasche">John Pasche</a>, was introduced in 1971.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Sticky Fingers</i> was the first to feature the logo of Rolling Stones Records, which effectively became the band's logo. It consisted of a pair of lips with a lapping tongue. Critic Sean Egan has said of the logo, "Without using the Stones' name, it instantly conjures them, or at least Jagger, as well as a certain lasciviousness that is the Stones' own ... It quickly and deservedly became the most famous logo in the history of popular music."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEgan2013_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEgan2013-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> The tongue and lips design was part of a package that, in 2003, <a href="/wiki/VH1" title="VH1">VH1</a> named the "No. 1 Greatest Album Cover" of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The album contains one of their best known hits, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_Sugar_(song)" title="Brown Sugar (song)">Brown Sugar</a>", and the <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country</a>-influenced "<a href="/wiki/Dead_Flowers_(The_Rolling_Stones_song)" title="Dead Flowers (The Rolling Stones song)">Dead Flowers</a>". Both were recorded at <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Muscle_Shoals_Sound_Studio" title="Muscle Shoals Sound Studio">Muscle Shoals Sound Studio</a> during the 1969 American tour. The album continued the band's immersion into heavily blues-influenced compositions. The album is noted for its "loose, ramshackle ambience"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> and marked Mick Taylor's first full release with the band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following the release of <i>Sticky Fingers</i>, the Rolling Stones left England after receiving financial advice. They moved to the South of France, where Richards rented the <a href="/wiki/Nellc%C3%B4te" title="Nellcôte">Villa Nellcôte</a> and sublet rooms to band members and entourage. Using the <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stones_Mobile_Studio" title="Rolling Stones Mobile Studio">Rolling Stones Mobile Studio</a>, they held recording sessions in the basement; they completed the resulting tracks, along with material dating as far back as 1969, at Sunset Studios in Los Angeles. The resulting double album, <i><a href="/wiki/Exile_on_Main_St." title="Exile on Main St.">Exile on Main St.</a></i> (UK number 1; US 1), was released in May 1972. Given an A+ grade by critic Robert Christgau<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> and disparaged by Lester Bangs – who reversed his opinion within months – <i>Exile</i> is now accepted as one of the Stones' best albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> The films <i><a href="/wiki/Cocksucker_Blues" title="Cocksucker Blues">Cocksucker Blues</a></i> (never officially released) and <i><a href="/wiki/Ladies_and_Gentlemen:_The_Rolling_Stones" title="Ladies and Gentlemen: The Rolling Stones">Ladies and Gentlemen: The Rolling Stones</a></i> (released in 1974) document the subsequent highly publicised <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_American_Tour_1972" title="The Rolling Stones American Tour 1972">1972 North American ("STP") Tour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1973.E2.80.9377:_Mid_seventies">1973–77: Mid seventies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Rolling_Stones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1973–77: Mid seventies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In November 1972, the band began sessions in <a href="/wiki/Kingston,_Jamaica" title="Kingston, Jamaica">Kingston, Jamaica</a>, for their follow-up to <i>Exile</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Goats_Head_Soup" title="Goats Head Soup">Goats Head Soup</a></i> (UK 1; US 1) (1973). The album spawned the worldwide hit "<a href="/wiki/Angie_(song)" title="Angie (song)">Angie</a>", but proved the first in a string of commercially successful but tepidly received studio albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> The sessions for <i>Goats Head Soup</i> led to a number of outtakes, most notably an early version of the popular ballad "<a href="/wiki/Waiting_on_a_Friend" title="Waiting on a Friend">Waiting on a Friend</a>", not released until <i><a href="/wiki/Tattoo_You" title="Tattoo You">Tattoo You</a></i> eight years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="480" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg/170px-Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg/255px-Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg/340px-Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bill_Wyman_-_Rolling_Stones_-_1975.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bill Wyman, 1975.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The making of the record was interrupted by another legal battle over drugs, dating back to their stay in France; a warrant for Richards' arrest had been issued, and the other band members had to return briefly to France for questioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002408_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002408-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> This, along with Jagger's convictions on drug charges (in 1967 and 1970<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002361_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002361-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup>), complicated the band's plans for their <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_Pacific_Tour_1973" title="The Rolling Stones Pacific Tour 1973">Pacific tour</a> in early 1973: they were denied permission to play in Japan and almost banned from Australia. This was followed by a <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones_European_Tour_1973" title="The Rolling Stones European Tour 1973">European tour</a> (bypassing France) in September/October 1973 – prior to which Richards had been arrested once more on drug charges, this time in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWyman2002412_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWyman2002412-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The band went to Musicland studios in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> to record their next album, 1974's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/It%27s_Only_Rock_%27n%27_Roll" title="It's Only Rock 'n' Roll">It's Only Rock 'n' Roll</a></i> (UK 2; US 1), but <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Miller" title="Jimmy Miller">Jimmy Miller</a>, who had drug abuse issues, was no longer producer. Instead, Jagger and Richards assumed production duties and were credited as "the Glimmer Twins".
